0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/16px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="16" height="8" class="noviewer" href="/wiki/Portal:United_Kingdom" title="Portal:United Kingdom">United Kingdom&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template:History of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template talk:History of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Kingdom of Great Britain</b>, officially called simply <b>Great Britain</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-Act_of_Union_1707_article_I_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Act_of_Union_1707_article_I-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-name_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-name-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> from 1 May 1707 to 31 December 1800.  The state came into being following the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> in 1706, ratified by the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a>, which united the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> (which included <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> to form a single kingdom encompassing the whole island of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and its outlying islands, with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a>.  It also did not include <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>, which remained a separate <a href="/wiki/Realm" title="Realm">realm</a>.  The <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary state</a> was governed by a single <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">parliament</a> and government that was based in <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Westminster</a>.  The former kingdoms had been in <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> since <a href="/wiki/James_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI">James VI</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_of_Scots" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Scots">King of Scots</a>, became <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/King_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Ireland">King of Ireland</a> in 1603 following the death of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth I">Queen Elizabeth I</a>, bringing about the "<a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a>". Also after the accession of <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">King George I</a> to the throne of Great Britain in 1714, the kingdom was in a personal union with the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Hanover" class="mw-redirect" title="Electorate of Hanover">Electorate of Hanover</a>.\n</p><p>The early years of the unified kingdom were marked by <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_risings" title="Jacobite risings">Jacobite risings</a> which ended in defeat for the Stuart cause at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden" title="Battle of Culloden">Culloden</a> in 1746. In 1763, victory in the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years&#39; War">Seven Years\' War</a> led to the dominance of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, which was to become the foremost global power for over a century and slowly grew to become the largest empire in history.\n</p><p>The Kingdom of Great Britain was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> on 1 January 1801 with the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union 1800</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Britain_(place_name)" title="Britain (place name)">Britain (place name)</a></div><p>The name <i>Britain</i> descends from the Latin name for the island of Great Britain, <i>Britannia</i> or <i>Britt\xc4\x81nia</i>, the land of the Britons via the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>Bretaigne</i> (whence also <a href="/wiki/Modern_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern French">Modern French</a> <i>Bretagne</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> <i>Bretayne</i>, <i>Breteyne</i>. The term <i>Great Britain</i> was first used officially in 1474.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The use of the word "Great" before "Britain" originates in the French language, which uses <i>Bretagne</i> for both Britain and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>. French therefore distinguishes between the two by calling Britain <i>la Grande Bretagne</i>, a distinction which was transferred into English.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union</a> state that England and Scotland were to be "United into One Kingdom by the Name of Great Britain",<sup id="cite_ref-Acts_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acts-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and as such "Great Britain" was the official name of the state, as well as being used in titles such as "Parliament of Great Britain".<sup id="cite_ref-name_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-name-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-name2_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-name2-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-name3_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-name3-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Both the Acts and the Treaty describe the country as "One Kingdom" and a "United Kingdom", which has led some much later publications into the error of treating the "United Kingdom" as a name before it actually came into being in 1801.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The websites of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="British Broadcasting Corporation">BBC</a>, and others, including the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Association" title="Historical Association">Historical Association</a>, refer to the state created on 1 May 1707 as <i>the United Kingdom of Great Britain</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>United Kingdom</i> was sometimes used during the 18th century to describe the state, but was not its name.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_structure">Political structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Political structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Parliament of Great Britain</a></div>\n<p>The kingdoms of England and Scotland, both in existence from the 9th century (with England incorporating Wales in the 16th century), were separate states until 1707. However, they had come into a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> in 1603, when James VI of Scotland became king of England under the name of <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a> under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a> meant that the whole of the island of Great Britain was now ruled by a single monarch, who by virtue of holding the English crown also ruled over the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a>. Each of the three kingdoms maintained its own parliament and laws. Various smaller islands were in the king\'s domain, including the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a>.\n</p><p>This disposition changed dramatically when the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a> came into force, with a single unified <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom#After_the_1707_Acts_of_Union" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">Crown of Great Britain</a> and a single unified parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Ireland remained formally separate, with its own parliament, until the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union 1800</a>. The Union of 1707 provided for <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_British_throne" title="Succession to the British throne">a Protestant-only succession</a> to the throne in accordance with the English <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement</a> of 1701; rather than Scotland\'s <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Security_1704" title="Act of Security 1704">Act of Security</a> of 1704, which ceased to have effect. The Act of Settlement required that the heir to the English throne be a descendant of the <a href="/wiki/Electress_Sophia_of_Hanover" class="mw-redirect" title="Electress Sophia of Hanover">Electress Sophia of Hanover</a> and not be a Catholic; this brought about the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">Hanoverian succession of George I</a> in 1714.\n</p><p>Legislative power was vested in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Parliament of Great Britain</a>, which replaced both the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In practice it was a continuation of the English parliament, sitting at the same location in Westminster, expanded to include representation from Scotland.  As with the former Parliament of England and the modern <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a>, the Parliament of Great Britain was formally constituted of three elements: the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_Great_Britain" title="House of Commons of Great Britain">House of Commons</a>, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a>. The right of the <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_England" title="Peerage of England">English peerage</a> to sit in the House of Lords remained unchanged, while the disproportionately large <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_Scotland" title="Peerage of Scotland">Scottish peerage</a> was permitted to send only 16 <a href="/wiki/Representative_peer" title="Representative peer">representative peers</a>, elected from amongst their number for the life of each parliament. Similarly, the members of the former English House of Commons continued as members of the British House of Commons, but as a reflection of the relative tax bases of the two countries the number of Scottish representatives was reduced to 45. Newly created peers in the <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_Great_Britain" title="Peerage of Great Britain">Peerage of Great Britain</a> were given the automatic right to sit in the Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>  Despite the end of a separate parliament for Scotland, it retained its own laws and system of courts, As its own established Presbyterian Church, and control over its own schools.  The social structure was highly hierarchical, and the same elite remain in control after 1707.<sup id="cite_ref-Williams,_1962_pp_271\xe2\x80\x9387_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams,_1962_pp_271\xe2\x80\x9387-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Scotland continued to have its own excellent universities, and with the strong intellectual community, especially in Edinburgh, The Scottish Enlightenment had a major impact on British, American and European thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_Ireland">Role of Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Role of Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As a result of <a href="/wiki/Poynings%27_Law" title="Poynings&#39; Law">Poynings\' Law</a> of 1495, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Parliament of Ireland</a> was subordinate to the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a>, and after 1707 to the Parliament of Great Britain. The Westminster parliament\'s <a href="/wiki/Declaratory_Act_1719" title="Declaratory Act 1719">Declaratory Act 1719</a> (also called the Dependency of Ireland on Great Britain Act 1719) noted that the <a href="/wiki/Irish_House_of_Lords" title="Irish House of Lords">Irish House of Lords</a> had recently "assumed to themselves a Power and Jurisdiction to examine, correct and amend" judgements of the Irish courts and declared that as the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> was subordinate to and dependent upon the crown of Great Britain, the <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">King</a>, through the Parliament of Great Britain, had "full power and authority to make laws and statutes of sufficient validity to bind the Kingdom and people of Ireland".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Act was repealed by the <a href="/wiki/Repeal_of_Act_for_Securing_Dependence_of_Ireland_Act_1782" title="Repeal of Act for Securing Dependence of Ireland Act 1782">Repeal of Act for Securing Dependence of Ireland Act 1782</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The same year, the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_1782" title="Constitution of 1782">Irish constitution of 1782</a> produced a period of legislative freedom. However, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">Irish Rebellion of 1798</a>, which sought to end the subordination and dependency upon the British crown and establish a republic, was one of the factors that led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> in 1801.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Merging_of_Scottish_and_English_Parliaments">Merging of Scottish and English Parliaments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Merging of Scottish and English Parliaments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The deeper political integration of her kingdoms was a key policy of <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a>, the last Stuart monarch of England and Scotland and the first monarch of Great Britain. A <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> was agreed in 1706 following negotiations between representatives of the parliaments of England and Scotland, and each parliament then passed separate Acts of Union to ratify it. The Acts came into effect on 1 May 1707, uniting the separate Parliaments and crowns of England and Scotland and forming a single Kingdom of Great Britain. Anne became the first monarch to occupy the unified British throne, and in line with Article 22 of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a>, Scotland and England each sent members to the new <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_Great_Britain" title="House of Commons of Great Britain">House of Commons of Great Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Williams,_1962_pp_271\xe2\x80\x9387_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams,_1962_pp_271\xe2\x80\x9387-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>  Scottish and English elites retained power, and each kept its legal and educational systems, as well as its established Church. United they formed a larger economy, and the Scots began to provide soldiers and colonial officials to the new British forces and its Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> However, in practice the new Scottish members and representative peers were elected by the outgoing Parliament of Scotland, while all existing members of the Houses of Commons and Lords at Westminster remained in office.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Queen_Anne.2C_1702.E2.80.931714"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Queen_Anne,_1702\xe2\x80\x931714">Queen Anne, 1702\xe2\x80\x931714</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Queen Anne, 1702\xe2\x80\x931714">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Anne, Queen of Great Britain</a></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</a> (1702\xe2\x80\x9314) England continued its policy of forming and funding alliances, especially with the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> against their common enemy, <a href="/wiki/King_Louis_XIV" class="mw-redirect" title="King Louis XIV">King Louis XIV</a> of France.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a>, who reigned 1702\xe2\x80\x931714, was the central decision maker, working closely with her advisers, especially her remarkably successful senior general, <a href="/wiki/John_Churchill,_1st_Duke_of_Marlborough" title="John Churchill, 1st Duke of Marlborough">John Churchill, 1st Duke of Marlborough</a>. The war was a financial drain, for Britain had to finance its allies and hire foreign soldiers. Stalemate on the battlefield and war weariness on the home front set in toward the end. The anti-war Tory politicians won control of Parliament in 1710 and forced a peace. The concluding <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Utrecht" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Utrecht">Treaty of Utrecht</a> was highly favourable for Britain. Spain lost its empire in Europe and faded away as a great power, while working to better manage its colonies in the Americas. The First British Empire, based upon the <a href="/wiki/English_overseas_possessions" title="English overseas possessions">English overseas possessions</a>, was enlarged. From France, Great Britain gained <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acadia" title="Acadia">Acadia</a>, and from Spain <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menorca" title="Menorca">Menorca</a>. Gibraltar became a major naval base which allowed Great Britain to control the entrance from the Atlantic to the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The war marks the weakening of French military, diplomatic and economic dominance, and the arrival on the world scene of Britain as a major imperial, military and financial power.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>  British historian <a href="/wiki/G._M._Trevelyan" title="G. M. Trevelyan">G. M. Trevelyan</a> argues: \n</p>\n<dl><dd>That Treaty [of Utrecht], which ushered in the stable and characteristic period of Eighteenth-Century civilization, marked the end of danger to Europe from the old French monarchy, and it marked a change of no less significance to the world at large,\xe2\x80\x94the maritime, commercial and financial supremacy of Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h2><span id="Hanoverian_succession:_1714.E2.80.931760"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hanoverian_succession:_1714\xe2\x80\x931760">Hanoverian succession: 1714\xe2\x80\x931760</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Hanoverian succession: 1714\xe2\x80\x931760">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the United Kingdom">History of the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgian_era" title="Georgian era">Georgian era</a></div>\n<p>In the 18th century England, and after 1707 Great Britain, rose to become the world\'s dominant <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonial power</a>, with France as its main rival on the imperial stage.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The pre-1707 <a href="/wiki/English_overseas_possessions" title="English overseas possessions">English overseas possessions</a> became the nucleus of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">First British Empire</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="George_I:_1714.E2.80.931727"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="George_I:_1714\xe2\x80\x931727">George I: 1714\xe2\x80\x931727</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: George I: 1714\xe2\x80\x931727">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">House of Hanover</a></div>\n<p>"In 1714 the ruling class was so bitterly divided that many feared a civil war might break out on Queen Anne\'s death", says historian W. A. Speck.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>  A couple of hundred of the richest landed elite families controlled politics, but they were deeply split, with Tories committed to the legitimacy of the <a href="/wiki/James_Francis_Edward_Stuart" title="James Francis Edward Stuart">Stuart "Old Pretender"</a>, then in exile. The Whigs strongly supported the king in order to uphold the Protestant succession. The new King George I was an unpopular German, and had only a small English army to support him. He did have military support from his native Hanover, and from his allies in the Netherlands. The <a href="/wiki/John_Erskine,_Earl_of_Mar_(1675%E2%80%931732)" title="John Erskine, Earl of Mar (1675\xe2\x80\x931732)">Earl of Mar</a> led 18 peers with 10,000 men in the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_rising_of_1715" title="Jacobite rising of 1715">Jacobite rising of 1715</a> based in Scotland which aimed to overthrow the king. It was poorly organised, and was decisively defeated, with several leaders executed, many leaders dispossessed of their lands, and 700 prominent followers deported to labour in the West Indies sugar plantations. A key decision was the refusal of the Pretender to change his religion from Catholic to Anglican, which probably would have mobilised much more of the Tory element. The Whigs came to power under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/James_Stanhope,_1st_Earl_Stanhope" title="James Stanhope, 1st Earl Stanhope">James Stanhope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Townshend,_2nd_Viscount_Townshend" title="Charles Townshend, 2nd Viscount Townshend">Charles Townshend</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Spencer,_3rd_Earl_of_Sunderland" title="Charles Spencer, 3rd Earl of Sunderland">Earl of Sunderland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Robert Walpole</a>. One by one the Tories were driven out of national and local government, and new laws were passed to impose more national control. The right of habeas corpus was restricted; to reduce electoral frenzy, the <a href="/wiki/Septennial_Act_1715" class="mw-redirect" title="Septennial Act 1715">Septennial Act 1715</a> increased the maximum life of a parliament from three years to seven.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He spent only about half as much time abroad during his reign as did William III, who also reigned for 13 years.<sup id="cite_ref-&quot;&quot;ODNBGeorgeI&quot;&quot;_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&quot;&quot;ODNBGeorgeI&quot;&quot;-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Black_(historian)" title="Jeremy Black (historian)">Jeremy Black</a> has argued that the king wanted to spend even more time in Hanover: "His visits, in 1716, 1719, 1720, 1723 and 1725, were lengthy, and, in total, he spent a considerable part of his reign abroad. These visits were also occasions both for significant negotiations and for the exchange of information and opinion....The visits to Hanover also provided critics with the opportunity...to argue that British interests were being neglected....George could not speak English, and all relevant documents from his British ministers were translated into French for him....Few British ministers or diplomats...knew German, or could handle it in precise discussion."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>George I supported the expulsion of the Tories from power; they remained in the political wilderness until young King George III came to power in 1760 and replaced Whigs with Tories.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> George I has often been caricatured in the history books, but according to his biographer <a href="/wiki/Ragnhild_Hatton" title="Ragnhild Hatton">Ragnhild Hatton</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>on the whole he did well by Great Britain, guiding the country calmly and responsibly through the difficult postwar years and repeated invasions or threatened invasions.... He liked efficiency and expertise, and had long experience of running an orderly state....He cared for the quality of his ministers and his officers, army and naval, and the strength of the navy in fast ships grew during his reign....He showed political vision and ability in the way in which he used British power in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span id="Age_of_Walpole:_1721.E2.80.931760"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_Walpole:_1721\xe2\x80\x931760">Age of Walpole: 1721\xe2\x80\x931760</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Age of Walpole: 1721\xe2\x80\x931760">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Robert Walpole</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the United Kingdom">History of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Houghton_Hall_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Houghton_Hall_01.jpg/300px-Houghton_Hall_01.jpg" width="300" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Houghton_Hall_01.jpg/450px-Houghton_Hall_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Houghton_Hall_01.jpg/600px-Houghton_Hall_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Houghton_Hall_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walpole\'s grand estate at <a href="/wiki/Houghton_Hall" title="Houghton Hall">Houghton Hall</a> represents the patronage rewards he bestowed on himself. It housed his <a href="/wiki/Walpole_collection" title="Walpole collection">great art collection</a> and often hosted the English elite. The king made him Duke of Orford when he retired in 1742.</div></div></div>\n<p>Robert Walpole (1676\xe2\x80\x931745) was a son of the gentry who rose to control the House of Commons, manipulating the levers of British politics with rare genius, from 1721 to 1742. He was thus the first prime minister (the term itself was in use by 1727).  He was followed as prime minister by his two disciples, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Pelham" title="Henry Pelham">Henry Pelham</a> (1743\xe2\x80\x931754) and Pelham\'s brother <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pelham-Holles,_1st_Duke_of_Newcastle" title="Thomas Pelham-Holles, 1st Duke of Newcastle">the Duke of Newcastle</a> (1754\xe2\x80\x931762).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  Historian Clayton Roberts summarizes his new functions: \n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>He monopolized the counsels of the King, he closely superintended the administration, he ruthlessly controlled patronage, and he led the predominant party in Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Sea_Bubble">South Sea Bubble</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: South Sea Bubble">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/South_Sea_Bubble" class="mw-redirect" title="South Sea Bubble">South Sea Bubble</a></div>\n<p>Corporate stock was a new phenomenon, not well understood except for the strong gossip among financiers that fortunes could be made overnight. The South Sea Company, although originally set up to trade with the Spanish Empire, quickly turned most of its attention to very high risk financing, involving \xc2\xa330 million or 60% of the entire British national debt. It set up a scheme that invited stock owners to turn in their certificates for stock in the Company at a par value of \xc2\xa3100\xe2\x80\x94the idea was they would profit by the rising price of the stock. Everyone with connections wanted in on the bonanza\xe2\x80\x94and many other outlandish schemes found gullible takers. South Sea stock peaked at \xc2\xa31060 on 25 June 1720. Then the bubble burst, and by the end of September it had fallen to \xc2\xa3150. Hundreds of prominent men had borrowed to buy stock high; their "profits" had vanished but they were liable to repay the full amount of the loans. Many went bankrupt and many more lost heavily.\n</p><p>Confidence in the entire national financial and political system had collapsed. Parliament investigated and concluded that there was widespread fraud amongst the company directors and corruption in the Cabinet. Among Cabinet members implicated were the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Postmaster_General" title="Postmaster General">Postmaster General</a>, and a Secretary of State, as well as the two top leaders <a href="/wiki/James_Stanhope,_1st_Earl_Stanhope" title="James Stanhope, 1st Earl Stanhope">Lord Stanhope</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Spencer,_3rd_Earl_of_Sunderland" title="Charles Spencer, 3rd Earl of Sunderland">Lord Sunderland</a>. Walpole had dabbled in the speculation himself but was not a major player. He rose to the challenge, as the new <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Treasury" title="First Lord of the Treasury">First Lord of the Treasury</a>, of resolving the financial and political disaster. The economy was basically healthy, and the panic ended. Working with the financiers he successfully restored confidence in the system. However, public opinion, as shaped by the many prominent men who had lost so much money so quickly, demanded revenge. Walpole supervised the process, which removed all 33 company directors and stripped them of, on average, 82% of their wealth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The money went to the victims. The government bought the stock of the South Sea Company for \xc2\xa333 and sold it to the Bank of England and the East India Company, the only other two corporations big enough to handle the challenge. Walpole made sure that King George and his mistresses were not embarrassed, and by the margin of three votes he saved several key government officials from impeachment.\n</p><p>Stanhope and Sunderland died of natural causes, leaving Walpole alone as the dominant figure in British politics. The public hailed him as the saviour of the financial system, and historians credit him with rescuing the Whig government, and indeed the Hanoverian dynasty, from total disgrace.<sup id="cite_ref-Marshall,_pp_127\xe2\x80\x9330_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall,_pp_127\xe2\x80\x9330-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kleer165_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kleer165-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patronage_and_corruption">Patronage and corruption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Patronage and corruption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Walpole</a> was a master of the effective use of patronage, as were Pelham and Lord Newcastle. They each paid close attention to the work of bestowing upon their political allies high places, lifetime pensions, honours, lucrative government contracts, and help at election time. In turn the friends enabled them to control Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Thus in 1742, over 140 Members of Parliament held powerful positions thanks in part to Walpole, including 24 men at the royal court, 50 in the government agencies, and the rest with <a href="/wiki/Sinecure" title="Sinecure">sinecures</a> or other handsome emoluments, often in the range of \xc2\xa3500 - \xc2\xa31000 per year. Usually there was little or no work involved. Walpole also distributed highly attractive ecclesiastical appointments. When the Court in 1725 instituted a new order of chivalry, the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Bath" title="Order of the Bath">Order of the Bath</a>, Walpole immediately seized the opportunity. He made sure that most of the 36 new honorees were peers and MPs who would provide him with useful connections.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Walpole himself became enormously wealthy, investing heavily in his estate at <a href="/wiki/Houghton_Hall" title="Houghton Hall">Houghton Hall</a> and its large collection of European master paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Walpole\'s methods won him victory after victory, but aroused furious opposition. Historian J. H. Plumb says: \n</p>\n<dl><dd>Walpole\'s policy had bred distrust, his methods hatred. Time and time again his policy was successful in Parliament only because of the government\'s absolute control of the Scottish members in the Commons and the Bishops in the Lords. He gave point to the opposition\'s cry that Walpole\'s policy was against the wishes of the nation, a policy imposed by a corrupt use of pension and place.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>The opposition called for "patriotism" and looked at the Prince of Wales as the future "Patriot King". Walpole supporters ridiculed the very term "patriot".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The opposition "<a href="/wiki/Country_Party_(Britain)" title="Country Party (Britain)">country party</a>" attacked Walpole relentlessly, primarily targeting patronage, which they denounced as immoral corruption. In turn Walpole imposed censorship on the London theatre and subsidised writers such as <a href="/wiki/William_Arnall" title="William Arnall">William Arnall</a> and others who rejected the charge of evil political corruption by arguing that corruption is the universal human condition. Furthermore, they argued, political divisiveness was also universal and inevitable because of selfish passions that were integral to human nature. Arnall argued that government must be strong enough to control conflict, and in that regard Walpole was quite successful. This style of "court" political rhetoric continued through the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>  Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Richard_Temple,_1st_Viscount_Cobham" title="Richard Temple, 1st Viscount Cobham">Lord Cobham</a>, a leading soldier, used his own connections to build up an opposition after 1733. Young <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Elder" class="mw-redirect" title="William Pitt the Elder">William Pitt</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Grenville" title="George Grenville">George Grenville</a> joined <a href="/wiki/Cobhamite" class="mw-redirect" title="Cobhamite">Cobham\'s faction</a>\xe2\x80\x94they were called "Cobham\'s Cubs". They became leading enemies of Walpole and both later became prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1741, Walpole was facing mounting criticism on foreign policy\xe2\x80\x94he was accused of entangling Britain in a useless war with Spain\xe2\x80\x94and mounting allegations of corruption. On 13 February 1741, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sandys,_1st_Baron_Sandys" title="Samuel Sandys, 1st Baron Sandys">Samuel Sandys</a>, a former ally, called for his removal.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> He said:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Such has been the conduct of Sir Robert Walpole, with regard to foreign affairs: he has deserted our allies, aggrandized our enemies, betrayed our commerce, and endangered our colonies; and yet this is the least criminal part of his ministry. For what is the loss of allies to the alienation of the people from the government, or the diminution of trade to the destruction of our liberties?<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Walpole\'s allies defeated a censure motion by a vote of 209 to 106, but Walpole\'s coalition lost seats in the election of 1741 and by a narrow margin he was finally forced out of power in early 1742.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Walpole.27s_foreign_policy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Walpole\'s_foreign_policy">Walpole\'s foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Walpole&#039;s foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/International_relations,_1648%E2%80%931814" title="International relations, 1648\xe2\x80\x931814">International relations, 1648\xe2\x80\x931814</a> and <a href="/wiki/France%E2%80%93United_Kingdom_relations" title="France\xe2\x80\x93United Kingdom relations">France\xe2\x80\x93United Kingdom relations</a></div>\n<p>Walpole secured widespread support with his policy of avoiding war.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> He used his influence to prevent George II from entering the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Polish_Succession" title="War of the Polish Succession">War of the Polish Succession</a> in 1733, because it was a dispute between the Bourbons and the Habsburgs. He boasted, "There are 50,000 men slain in Europe this year, and not one Englishman."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>  Walpole himself let others, especially <a href="/wiki/Charles_Townshend,_2nd_Viscount_Townshend" title="Charles Townshend, 2nd Viscount Townshend">his brother-in-law Lord Townshend</a>, handle foreign policy until about 1726, then took charge. A major challenge for his administration was the royal role as simultaneous ruler of Hanover, a small German state that was opposed to Prussian supremacy. George I and George II saw a French alliance as the best way to neutralise Prussia. They forced a dramatic reversal of British foreign policy, which for centuries had seen France as England\'s greatest enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> However, the bellicose trouble-maker King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> died in 1715, and the regents who ran France were preoccupied with internal affairs. <a href="/wiki/Louis_XV_of_France" title="Louis XV of France">King Louis XV</a> came of age in 1726, and his elderly chief minister <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Hercule_de_Fleury" title="Andr\xc3\xa9-Hercule de Fleury">Cardinal Fleury</a> collaborated informally with Walpole to prevent a major war and keep the peace. Both sides wanted peace, which allowed both countries enormous cost savings, and recovery from expensive wars.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry Pelham became prime minister in 1744 and continued Walpole\'s policies. He worked for an end to the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Austrian_Succession" title="War of the Austrian Succession">War of the Austrian Succession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> His financial policy was a major success once peace had been signed in 1748. He demobilised the armed forces, and reduced government spending from \xc2\xa312 million to \xc2\xa37 million. He refinanced the national debt, dropping the interest rate from 4% p.a. to 3% p.a. Taxes had risen to pay for the war, but in 1752 he reduced the land tax from four shillings to two shillings in the pound: that is, from 20% to 10%.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lower_debt_and_taxes">Lower debt and taxes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lower debt and taxes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>By avoiding wars, Walpole could lower taxes.  He reduced the national debt with a sinking fund, and by negotiating lower interest rates.  He reduced the land tax from four shillings in 1721, to 3s in 1728, 2s in 1731 and finally to only 1s (i.e. 5%) in 1732. His long-term goal was to replace the land tax, which was paid by the local gentry, with excise and customs taxes, which were paid by merchants and ultimately by consumers. Walpole joked that the landed gentry resembled hogs, which squealed loudly whenever anyone laid hands on them. By contrast, he said, merchants were like sheep, and yielded their wool without complaint.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The joke backfired in 1733 when he was defeated in a major battle to impose excise taxes on wine and tobacco. To reduce the threat of smuggling, the tax was to be collected not at ports but at warehouses. This new proposal, however, was extremely unpopular with the public, and aroused the opposition of the merchants because of the supervision it would involve. Walpole was defeated as his strength in Parliament dropped a notch.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Walpole.27s_reputation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Walpole\'s_reputation">Walpole\'s reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Walpole&#039;s reputation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Stature_of_a_Great_Man_or_the_English_Colossus_cph.3b03411.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/The_Stature_of_a_Great_Man_or_the_English_Colossus_cph.3b03411.jpg/220px-The_Stature_of_a_Great_Man_or_the_English_Colossus_cph.3b03411.jpg" width="220" height="360" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/The_Stature_of_a_Great_Man_or_the_English_Colossus_cph.3b03411.jpg/330px-The_Stature_of_a_Great_Man_or_the_English_Colossus_cph.3b03411.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/The_Stature_of_a_Great_Man_or_the_English_Colossus_cph.3b03411.jpg/440px-The_Stature_of_a_Great_Man_or_the_English_Colossus_cph.3b03411.jpg 2x" data-file-width="913" data-file-height="1494" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Stature_of_a_Great_Man_or_the_English_Colossus_cph.3b03411.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1740 political cartoon depicting a towering Walpole as the <a href="/wiki/Colossus_of_Rhodes" title="Colossus of Rhodes">Colossus of Rhodes</a>.</div></div></div><p>\nHistorians hold Walpole\'s record in high regard, though there has been a recent tendency to share credit more widely among his allies. W.A. Speck says that Walpole\'s uninterrupted run of 20 years as Prime Minister</p><blockquote><p>is rightly regarded as one of the major feats of British political history...&#160;Explanations are usually offered in terms of his expert handling of the political system after 1720, [and] his unique blending of the surviving powers of the crown with the increasing influence of the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>He was a <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political party)">Whig</a> from the gentry class, who was first elected to Parliament in 1701, and held many senior positions.  He was a country squire and looked to country gentlemen for his political base. Historian Frank O\'Gorman says his leadership in Parliament reflected his "reasonable and persuasive oratory, his ability to move both the emotions as well as the minds of men, and, above all, his extraordinary self-confidence."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Hoppit says Walpole\'s policies sought moderation: he worked for peace, lower taxes, growing exports, and allowed a little more tolerance for Protestant Dissenters.  He avoided controversy and high-intensity disputes, as his middle way attracted moderates from both the Whig and Tory camps.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>  H.T. Dickinson sums up his historical role:</p><blockquote><p>Walpole was one of the greatest politicians in British history.  He played a significant role in sustaining the Whig party, safeguarding the Hanoverian succession, and defending the principles of the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> (1688)&#160;... He established a stable political supremacy for the Whig party and taught succeeding ministers how best to establish an effective working relationship between Crown and Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span id="Age_of_George_III.2C_1760.E2.80.931820"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_George_III,_1760\xe2\x80\x931820">Age of George III, 1760\xe2\x80\x931820</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Age of George III, 1760\xe2\x80\x931820">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Victory_in_the_Seven_Years.27_War.2C_1756.E2.80.931763"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Victory_in_the_Seven_Years\'_War,_1756\xe2\x80\x931763">Victory in the Seven Years\' War, 1756\xe2\x80\x931763</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Victory in the Seven Years&#039; War, 1756\xe2\x80\x931763">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years&#39; War">Seven Years\' War</a>, which began in 1756, was the first war waged on a global scale and saw <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_in_the_Seven_Years%27_War" title="Great Britain in the Seven Years&#39; War">British involvement</a> in Europe, <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_India" title="Company rule in India">India</a>, North America, the Caribbean, the Philippines, and coastal Africa. The results were highly favorable for Britain, and a major disaster for France.  Key decisions were largely in the hands of <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt,_1st_Earl_of_Chatham" title="William Pitt, 1st Earl of Chatham">William Pitt the Elder</a>. The war started poorly. Britain <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Fort_St_Philip_(1756)" title="Siege of Fort St Philip (1756)">lost the island of Minorca in 1756</a>, and suffered a series of defeats in North America.  After years of setbacks and mediocre results, British luck turned in the "miracle year" ("Annus Mirabilis") of 1759.  The British had entered the year anxious about a <a href="/wiki/Planned_French_Invasion_of_Britain_(1759)" class="mw-redirect" title="Planned French Invasion of Britain (1759)">French invasion</a>, but by the end of the year, they were victorious in all theatres.  In the Americas, they <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ticonderoga_(1759)" title="Battle of Ticonderoga (1759)">captured Fort Ticonderoga (Carillon)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forbes_Expedition" title="Forbes Expedition">drove the French out of the Ohio Country</a>, captured <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a> in Canada as a result of the decisive <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Plains_of_Abraham" title="Battle of the Plains of Abraham">Battle of the Plains of Abraham</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Guadeloupe_(1759)" title="Invasion of Guadeloupe (1759)">captured the rich sugar island of Guadeloupe</a> in the West Indies. In India, the John Company <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Madras" title="Siege of Madras">repulsed French forces besieging Madras</a>. In Europe, British troops partook in a decisive Allied victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Minden" title="Battle of Minden">Battle of Minden</a>. The victory over the French navy at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lagos" title="Battle of Lagos">Battle of Lagos</a> and the decisive <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Quiberon_Bay" title="Battle of Quiberon Bay">Battle of Quiberon Bay</a> ended threats of a French invasion, and confirmed Britain\'s reputation as the world\'s foremost naval power.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">Treaty of Paris of 1763</a> marked the high point of the First British Empire. France\'s future in North America ended, as <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a> (Quebec) came under British control. In India, the <a href="/wiki/Carnatic_Wars#Third_Carnatic_War_(1756\xe2\x80\x931763)" title="Carnatic Wars">third Carnatic War</a> had left France still in control of several small <a href="/wiki/French_India" title="French India">enclaves</a>, but with military restrictions and an obligation to support the British client states, effectively leaving the future of India to Great Britain. The British victory over France in the Seven Years\' War therefore left Great Britain as the world\'s dominant colonial power, with a bitter France thirsting for revenge.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evangelical_religion_and_social_reform">Evangelical religion and social reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Evangelical religion and social reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The evangelical movement inside and outside the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> gained strength in the late 18th and early 19th century. The movement challenged the traditional religious sensibility that emphasized a code of honor for the upper-class, and suitable behaviour for everyone else, together with faithful observances of rituals. <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a> (1703\xe2\x80\x931791) and his followers preached revivalist religion, trying to convert individuals to a personal relationship with Christ through Bible reading, regular prayer, and especially the revival experience. Wesley himself preached 52,000 times, calling on men and women to "redeem the time" and save their souls. Wesley always operated inside the Church of England, but at his death, it set up outside institutions that became the <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodist Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>  It stood alongside the traditional nonconformist churches, Presbyterians, Congregationalist, Baptists, Unitarians and Quakers. The nonconformist churches, however, were less influenced by revivalism.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Church of England remained dominant, but it had a growing evangelical, revivalist faction the "Low Church". Its leaders included <a href="/wiki/William_Wilberforce" title="William Wilberforce">William Wilberforce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hannah_More" title="Hannah More">Hannah More</a>. It reached the upper class through the <a href="/wiki/Clapham_Sect" title="Clapham Sect">Clapham Sect</a>. It did not seek political reform, but rather the opportunity to save souls through political action by freeing slaves, abolishing the duel, prohibiting cruelty to children and animals, stopping gambling, avoiding frivolity on the Sabbath; they read the Bible every day. All souls were equal in God\'s view, but not all bodies, so evangelicals did not challenge the hierarchical structure of English society.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_British_Empire">First British Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: First British Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_British_Empire" title="Historiography of the British Empire">Historiography of the British Empire</a></div>\n<p>The first British Empire, was based largely in North America in the West Indies, with a growing presence in India. Emigration from Britain went mostly to the 13 American colonies, and to a lesser extent to Newfoundland and Nova Scotia. Very few permanent settlers went to India.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercantilist_Trade_Policy">Mercantilist Trade Policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mercantilist Trade Policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">Mercantilism</a> was the basic policy imposed by Great Britain on its overseas possessions.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Mercantilism meant that the government and the merchants became partners with the goal of increasing political power and private wealth, to the exclusion of other empires. The government protected its merchants\xe2\x80\x94and kept others out\xe2\x80\x94by trade barriers, regulations, and subsidies to domestic industries to maximise exports from and minimise imports to the realm. The government had to fight smuggling\xe2\x80\x94which became a favourite American technique in the 18th century to circumvent the restrictions on trading with the French, Spanish or Dutch. The goal of mercantilism was to run trade surpluses, so that gold and silver would pour into London. The government took its share through duties and taxes, with the remainder going to merchants in London and other British ports. The government spent much of its revenue on a superb Royal Navy, which not only protected the British colonies but threatened the colonies of the other empires, and sometimes seized them. Thus the Royal Navy captured <a href="/wiki/New_Amsterdam" title="New Amsterdam">New Amsterdam</a> (later New York) in 1664. The colonies were captive markets for British industry, and the goal was to enrich the mother country.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_the_13_American_colonies">Loss of the 13 American colonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Loss of the 13 American colonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a></div>\n<p>During the 1760s and 1770s, relations with the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> turned from benign neglect to outright revolt, primarily because of the British Parliament\'s insistence on taxing colonists without their consent.  In 1775, the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> began, as the Americans trapped the British army in Boston and suppressed the Loyalists who supported the Crown. In 1776 the Americans <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">declared the independence of the United States of America</a>. Under the military leadership of General <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, and, with economic and military assistance from France, the Dutch Republic, and Spain, the United States held off successive British invasions. The Americans captured two main British armies in 1777 and 1781. After that King George III lost control of Parliament and was unable to continue the war. It ended with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris</a> by which Great Britain relinquished the Thirteen Colonies and recognized the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. The war was expensive but the British financed it successfully.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_British_Empire">Second British Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Second British Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The loss of the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> marked the transition between the "first" and "second" empires, in which Britain shifted its attention away from the Americas to Asia, the Pacific and later Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Wealth_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Wealth of Nations">Wealth of Nations</a></i>, published in 1776, had argued that colonies were redundant, and that <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> should replace the old <a href="/wiki/Mercantilist" class="mw-redirect" title="Mercantilist">mercantilist</a> policies that had characterised the first period of colonial expansion, dating back to the protectionism of Spain and Portugal. The growth of trade between the newly independent United States and Great Britain after 1781<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> confirmed Smith\'s view that political control was not necessary for economic success.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Canada" title="History of Canada">History of Canada</a></div>\n<p>After a series of "French and Indian wars", the British took over most of France\'s North American operations in 1763. <a href="/wiki/Canada_(New_France)" title="Canada (New France)">New France</a> became <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec_(1763%E2%80%9391)" class="mw-redirect" title="Province of Quebec (1763\xe2\x80\x9391)">Quebec</a>. Great Britain\'s policy was to respect Quebec\'s Catholic establishment as well as its semi-feudal legal, economic, and social systems. By the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Act" title="Quebec Act">Quebec Act</a> of 1774, the Province of Quebec was enlarged to include the western holdings of the American colonies. In the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halifax,_Nova_Scotia" title="Halifax, Nova Scotia">Halifax, Nova Scotia</a> became Britain\'s major base for naval action. They repulsed an American revolutionary invasion in 1776, but in 1777 a British invasion army was captured in New York, encouraging France to enter the war.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the American victory, between 40,000 and 60,000 <a href="/wiki/United_Empire_Loyalist" title="United Empire Loyalist">defeated Loyalists</a> migrated, some bringing their slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Most families were given free land to compensate their losses. Several thousand free blacks also arrived; most of them later went to <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The 14,000 Loyalists who went to the Saint John and Saint Croix river valleys, then part of Nova Scotia, were not welcomed by the locals. Therefore, in 1784 the British split off <a href="/wiki/History_of_New_Brunswick" title="History of New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> as a separate colony. The Constitutional Act of 1791 created the provinces of Upper Canada (mainly English-speaking) and Lower Canada (mainly French-speaking) to defuse tensions between the French and English-speaking communities, and implemented governmental systems similar to those employed in Great Britain, with the intention of asserting imperial authority and not allowing the sort of popular control of government that was perceived to have led to the American Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Australia_(1788%E2%80%931850)" title="History of Australia (1788\xe2\x80\x931850)">History of Australia (1788\xe2\x80\x931850)</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Australia" title="History of Australia">History of Australia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_New_Zealand" title="History of New Zealand">History of New Zealand</a></div>\n<p>In 1770, British explorer <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> had discovered the eastern coast of Australia whilst on a scientific <a href="/wiki/First_voyage_of_James_Cook" title="First voyage of James Cook">voyage</a> to the South Pacific. In 1778, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Banks" title="Joseph Banks">Joseph Banks</a>, Cook\'s botanist on the voyage, presented evidence to the government on the suitability of <a href="/wiki/Botany_Bay" title="Botany Bay">Botany Bay</a> for the establishment of a penal settlement. Australia marks the beginning of the Second British Empire. It was planned by the government in London and designed as a replacement for the lost American colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The American Loyalist <a href="/wiki/James_Matra" title="James Matra">James Matra</a> in 1783 wrote "A Proposal for Establishing a Settlement in New South Wales" proposing the establishment of a colony composed of American Loyalists, Chinese and South Sea Islanders (but not convicts).<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Matra reasoned that the land was suitable for plantations of sugar, cotton and tobacco; New Zealand timber and hemp or flax could prove valuable commodities; it could form a base for Pacific trade; and it could be a suitable compensation for displaced American Loyalists. At the suggestion of Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Lord_Sydney" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Sydney">Lord Sydney</a>, Matra amended his proposal to include convicts as settlers, considering that this would benefit both "Economy to the Publick, &amp; Humanity to the Individual". The government adopted the basics of Matra\'s plan in 1784, and funded the settlement of convicts.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1787 the <a href="/wiki/First_Fleet" title="First Fleet">First Fleet</a> set sail, carrying the first shipment of <a href="/wiki/Convictism_in_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Convictism in Australia">convicts</a> to the colony. It arrived in January 1788.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clive.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Clive.jpg/250px-Clive.jpg" width="250" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Clive.jpg/375px-Clive.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Clive.jpg/500px-Clive.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1870" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clive.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lord_Clive" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Clive">Lord Clive</a> of the East India Company meeting his ally <a href="/wiki/Mir_Jafar" title="Mir Jafar">Mir Jafar</a> after their decisive victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plassey" title="Battle of Plassey">Battle of Plassey</a> in 1757</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> was not directly ruled by the British government, instead certain parts were seized by the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a>, a private, for-profit corporation, with its own army. The "John Company" (as it was nicknamed) took direct control of half of India and built friendly relations with the other half, which was controlled by numerous local princes. Its goal was trade, and vast profits for the Company officials, not the building of the British empire. Company interests expanded during the 18th century to include control of territory as the old <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> declined in power and the East India Company battled for the spoils with the <a href="/wiki/French_East_India_Company" title="French East India Company">French East India Company</a> (<i>Compagnie fran\xc3\xa7aise des Indes orientales</i>) during the <a href="/wiki/Carnatic_Wars" title="Carnatic Wars">Carnatic Wars</a> of the 1740s and 1750s. Victories at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plassey" title="Battle of Plassey">Battle of Plassey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Buxar" title="Battle of Buxar">Battle of Buxar</a> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Clive" title="Robert Clive">Robert Clive</a> gave the Company control over <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Presidency" title="Bengal Presidency">Bengal</a> and made it the major military and political power in India. In the following decades it gradually increased the extent of territories under its control, ruling either directly or in cooperation with local princes. Although Britain itself only had a small standing army, the Company had a large and well trained force, the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_armies" title="Presidency armies">presidency armies</a>, with British officers commanding native Indian troops (called <a href="/wiki/Sepoys" class="mw-redirect" title="Sepoys">sepoys</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Battling_the_French_Revolution_and_Napoleon">Battling the French Revolution and Napoleon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Battling the French Revolution and Napoleon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_First_Coalition" title="War of the First Coalition">War of the First Coalition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Second_Coalition" title="War of the Second Coalition">War of the Second Coalition</a></div>\n<p>With the regicide of King Louis XVI in 1793, the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> represented a contest of ideologies between conservative, royalist Britain and radical Republican France.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>  The long bitter wars with France 1793-1815, saw anti-Catholicism emerge as the glue that held the three kingdoms together. From the upper classes to the lower classes, Protestants were brought together from England, Scotland and Ireland into a profound distrust and distaste for all things French. That enemy nation was depicted as the natural home of misery and oppression because of its inherent inability to shed the darkness of Catholic superstition and clerical manipulation.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleon">Napoleon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Napoleon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>It was not only Britain\'s position on the world stage that was threatened: Napoleon, who came to power in 1799, threatened invasion of Great Britain itself, and with it, a fate similar to the countries of continental Europe that his armies had overrun. The <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> were therefore ones in which the British invested all the moneys and energies it could raise. French ports were blockaded by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The French Revolution revived religious and political grievances in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>. In 1798, Irish nationalists, under Protestant leadership, plotted the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">Irish Rebellion of 1798</a>, believing that the French would help them to overthrow the British.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>. They hoped for significant French support, which never came. The uprising was very poorly organized, and quickly suppressed by much more powerful British forces.
